*Definition.* Given a $\bar{p}$-smooth $(\bar{p} > 1)$ translation invariant state $\{f_\Lambda\}$ and an interaction polynomial, $P$, we define the *mean interaction* associated to the region $\Lambda$ by
$$ \rho_\Lambda(f, P) = \frac{1}{|\Lambda|} \int_Q U_\Lambda f_\Lambda d\mu_0 . $$

The strict locality of the interaction makes the control of the infinite volume "limit" of $\rho_\Lambda$ especially easy:

***

# Page 62 (Journal Page 251)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 251

PROPOSITION VII.13. $\rho_\Lambda(f, P)$ is a number $\rho(f, P)$ independent of $\Lambda$.

*Proof.* Since $\Lambda \to U_\Lambda$ is $L^p$ continuous for $p < \infty$ (cf. Theorem II.10), $f_\Lambda$ is in $L^q (q \le \bar{p})$ and, by consistency,
$$ \rho_\Lambda(f, P) = \frac{1}{|\Lambda|} \int_Q U_\Lambda f_{\Lambda'} d\mu_0 $$
if $\Lambda \subset \Lambda', \rho_\Lambda$ is clearly continuous. Moreover, if $\Lambda_1 \cup \Lambda_2 = \Lambda$ and $\Lambda_1 \cap \Lambda_2 = \emptyset$, then
$$ |\Lambda| \rho_\Lambda = \int_Q (U_{\Lambda_1} + U_{\Lambda_2}) f_\Lambda d\mu_0 = |\Lambda_1| \rho_{\Lambda_1} + |\Lambda_2| \rho_{\Lambda_2} . $$
It now follows easily that $\rho_\Lambda$ is independent of $\Lambda$. $\square$

The following half of the Gibbs variational principle is now straightforward:

THEOREM VII.14 (Gibbs variational inequality). *For any semibounded interaction polynomial $P$, and any weakly tempered translation invariant state $\{f_\Lambda\}$,*
$$ s(f) - \rho(f, P) \le \alpha_\infty(P) . $$

*Proof.* By the concavity of $\ln x$ and the normalization of the $f_\Lambda$ we have for any finite $\Lambda$:
$$ \begin{aligned} |\Lambda|^{-1} S_\Lambda(f) - \rho_\Lambda(f, P) &= |\Lambda|^{-1} \int_Q f_\Lambda [- U_\Lambda - \ln f_\Lambda] d\mu_0 \\ &= |\Lambda|^{-1} \int_Q f_\Lambda \ln [e^{-U_\Lambda} / f_\Lambda] d\mu_0 \\ &\le |\Lambda|^{-1} \ln \left( \int_Q e^{-U_\Lambda} d\mu_0 \right) = |\Lambda|^{-1} \ln Z_\Lambda . \end{aligned} $$
Taking $\Lambda \to \infty$ (Fisher), we complete the proof. $\square$

Except for the exactly soluble linear and quadratic interactions (where the variational equality can be verified by direct computation) we have nothing to report about the variational *equality* for two-dimensional theories. This is not surprising, since we know the existence of infinite volume states only for small coupling constant [70] and we do not even know weak temperedness holds in that case. However, for the one-dimensional case, we can verify the variational equality directly by the use of transfer matrix ideas. Let $\Omega_Q$ be the ground state for the Hamiltonian $H_Q = H_0 + :Q(q):$ and let $E_Q$ be the ground state energy. Finally let $f^Q$ be the associated Euclidean state so that (cf. Theorem II.18)
$$ f_{[a,b]}^Q = (J_a \Omega_Q)(J_b \Omega_Q) \exp \left( -\int_a^b :Q(q(t)): dt \right) \exp ((b - a) E_Q) . \eqno{\text{(VII.18)}} $$

***

# Page 63 (Journal Page 252)

252 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

Explicit computation now yields

THEOREM VII.15. *For the one-dimensional Markov theory, we have:*
(i) $\rho(f^Q, P) = (\Omega_Q, :P(q): \Omega_Q)$;
(ii) $s(f^Q) = - (\Omega_Q, H_0 \Omega_Q)$;
(iii) $s(f^Q) - \rho(f^Q, Q) = \alpha_\infty(Q) = - E_Q$.

*Proof.* If $a < c < b$, then
$$ \begin{aligned} \int :P(q(c)): f_{[a,b]}^Q d\mu_0 &= \langle e^{-(c-a)(H_Q-E_Q)} \Omega_Q, :P(q): e^{-(b-c)(H_Q-E_Q)} \Omega_Q \rangle \\ &= \langle \Omega_Q, :P(q): \Omega_Q \rangle \end{aligned} $$
which proves (i). By a similar computation using (VII.18), we obtain
$$ \begin{aligned} -\int_{[a,b]} \ln f_{[a,b]}^Q d\mu_0 &= -\int (J_a \Omega_Q)(J_b \Omega_Q) \ln (J_a \Omega_Q)(J_b \Omega_Q) d\mu_0 \\ &\quad + |b - a| (\langle \Omega_Q, :Q: \Omega_Q \rangle - E_Q) . \end{aligned} $$
The first term on the right can be dominated by
$$ 2 | \langle \Omega_Q, e^{-(b-a)H_0} \Omega_Q \ln \Omega_Q \rangle | \le 2 \| \Omega_Q \| \cdot \| \Omega_Q \ln \Omega_Q \| < \infty . $$
Dividing by $|b - a|$ and going to infinity, we see that
$$ s(f^Q) = \langle \Omega_Q, :Q: \Omega_Q \rangle - E_Q , $$
and (ii) and (iii) follow immediately. $\square$

We emphasize two things about these explicit computations. First, let us translate the Gibbs inequality $s(f^Q) - \rho(f^Q, P) \le \alpha_\infty(P)$ to time zero expectations. Using Theorem VII.15 we see it is equivalent to
$$ -(\Omega_Q, (H_0 + :P:) \Omega_Q) \le -E_P . $$
Thus the Gibbs variational inequality for the states $\{f^Q\}$ is equivalent to the Rayleigh-Ritz variational inequality for the vectors $\{\Omega_Q\}$. It is also worth emphasizing the content of (ii); namely, entropy density is the Euclidean version of the free Hamiltonian expectation value. In the case of non-relativistic quantum mechanics, general results of this nature have been recently obtained by W. Crutchfield [9].

This suggests a variety of alternative pictures for the two-dimensional case. Let us mention one of them. By results of Glimm-Jaffe [33] and Spencer [97], we know that in certain infinite volume (Hamiltonian) limits, both $(\Omega, T_0^{00} \Omega)$ and $(\Omega, T_I^{00} \Omega)$ exist where $T_0^{00}$ is the free energy density and $T_I^{00} = :P(\phi(x)):$. Let $f$ be the associated Euclidean state. Then:

*Conjecture.* $s(f) = - (\Omega, T_0^{00} \Omega)$ and $\alpha_\infty(P) = - (\Omega, T^{00} \Omega)$ where $T^{00} = T_0^{00} + T_I^{00}$. (Note: If we know that $f$ is weakly tempered, it is easy to prove

***

# Page 64 (Journal Page 253)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 253

that $\rho(f, P) = (\Omega, T_I^{00} \Omega)$.) Moreover, one has the following Rayleigh-Ritz principle: the energy density associated with the interaction $:P:$ for translation invariant locally Fock states of the Hamiltonian theory is minimized precisely by the infinite volume (Hamiltonian) states for the $P(\phi)_2$ theory and the minimum value is $-\alpha_\infty(P)$. Were such a variational principle established, it might become a valuable calculational tool.

Finally, we wish to go part way towards a proof that every weakly tempered, translation invariant, Gibbsian state $f$ for interaction $P$ satisfies the variational equality $s(f) - \rho(f, P) = \alpha_\infty(P)$. If $f$ is $\Lambda$-Gibbsian then we claim that
$$ f_\Lambda = e^{-U_\Lambda} \psi_{\partial\Lambda} / E_{\partial\Lambda}(e^{-U_\Lambda}) \eqno{\text{(VII.19)}} $$
where $E_{\partial\Lambda}$ is the free field conditional expectation. For by Theorem VII.2, $f_\Lambda = e^{-U_\Lambda} \psi_{\partial\Lambda}$ from which it follows that $\psi_{\partial\Lambda} = E_{\partial\Lambda}(f_\Lambda) = E_{\partial\Lambda}(e^{-U_\Lambda}) \psi_{\partial\Lambda}$.
Now suppose we define the *conditional partition function*
$$ \tilde{Z}_{\partial\Lambda} = E_{\partial\Lambda}(e^{-U_\Lambda}) , $$
the *conditional pressure*,
$$ \tilde{p}_{\partial\Lambda} = \frac{1}{|\Lambda|} \ln \tilde{Z}_{\partial\Lambda} , $$
and the *boundary entropy*,
$$ S_{\partial\Lambda} = - \int_Q f_{\partial\Lambda} \ln f_{\partial\Lambda} d\mu_0 , $$
where $\tilde{Z}_{\partial\Lambda}$ and $\tilde{p}_{\partial\Lambda}$ are $\Sigma_{\partial\Lambda}$-measurable functions. Then

PROPOSITION VII.16. *For any Gibbsian state $f$,*
$$ \frac{S_\Lambda(f)}{|\Lambda|} = \rho_\Lambda(f, P) + \frac{1}{|\Lambda|} S_{\partial\Lambda} + \int \tilde{p}_{\partial\Lambda} d\bar{\mu} . \eqno{\text{(VII.20)}} $$

*Proof.* By (VII.19) $\ln f_\Lambda = - U_\Lambda + \ln f_{\partial\Lambda} - \tilde{p}_{\partial\Lambda}$. Multiplying by $- |\Lambda|^{-1} f_\Lambda$ and integrating, we obtain (VII.20). $\square$

Thus to prove that a given Gibbsian state $f$ obeys the Gibbs variational equality we need only prove that (i) the conditional pressure converges to the pressure in a suitable sense, and (ii) the boundary entropy density vanishes. At present, we are unable to prove (i). As for (ii), we have

THEOREM VII.17. *Let $f$ be a weakly tempered, translation invariant state. Let $\Lambda_n \to \infty$ (Fisher). Then $(1/|\Lambda_n|) S_{\partial\Lambda_n}(f) \to 0$ as $n \to \infty$.*

*Proof.* Let $\eta < 1$ be the exponent in the weak temperedness condition on $f$. Choose $\eta' \in (\eta, 1)$ and $\Lambda'$ in accordance with Theorem C.3. By the monotonicity (Theorem VI.6b) and weak subadditivity of the entropy (Theorem VI.6c):

***

# Page 65 (Journal Page 254)

254 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

$$ S_{\Lambda_n}(f) \le S_{\Lambda'_n \cup \partial\Lambda_n}(f) \le S_{\Lambda'_n}(f) + S_{\partial\Lambda_n}(f) + \ln \| f_{\Lambda'_n} f_{\partial\Lambda_n} \|_1 . $$
By Lemma VI.8,
$$ \frac{1}{|\Lambda_n|} \ln \| f_{\Lambda'_n} f_{\partial\Lambda_n} \|_1 \longrightarrow 0 $$
since $d(\Lambda'_n, \partial\Lambda_n) \ge d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}$. Thus by Theorem VI.9,
$$ s(f) \le s(f) + \lim \frac{1}{|\Lambda_n|} S_{\partial\Lambda_n}(f) . $$
Since $S_{\partial\Lambda}(f) \le 0$ (Theorem VI.6a), we see that the limit in question is 0. $\square$

## Appendix A. Positive definite matrices with nonpositive off-diagonal elements

In this appendix, we summarize a few properties of two classes of matrices:

*Definitions.*
(i) $\mathcal{M}_n$ will denote the family of $n \times n$ invertible positive definite matrices with nonpositive off-diagonal elements.
(ii) $\mathcal{K}_n = \{ A \mid A^{-1} \in \mathcal{M}_n \}$.
(iii) $\mathcal{M}_\infty$ denotes the family of positive invertible operators on $l^2$ with nonpositive off-diagonal elements.
(iv) $\mathcal{K}_\infty = \{ A \mid A^{-1} \in \mathcal{M}_\infty \}$.

THEOREM A.1. *Any $A \in \mathcal{K}_n (n \le \infty)$ has nonnegative off-diagonal elements.*

*Proof.* Since $F \equiv A^{-1}$ is invertible, $F \ge cI$ for some $c > 0$. Write $F = D + B$ where $B$ vanishes on the diagonal and $D$ vanishes off the diagonal. Then $D \ge cI$ and for any $\lambda \in [0, 1], F(\lambda) \equiv D + \lambda B = \lambda F + (1 - \lambda) D$ is greater than $cI$. It follows that $F(\lambda)^{-1}$ is analytic in a neighbourhood of $[0, 1]$. But for $\lambda$ small, $F(\lambda)^{-1} = D^{-1} \sum_{n=0}^\infty (-\lambda B D^{-1})^n$. Since $B$ has only nonpositive elements and $D^{-1}$ only nonnegative elements, each matrix in the Neumann series above has only nonnegative elements. Thus each matrix element of $F(\lambda)^{-1}$ is analytic in a neighbourhood of $[0, 1]$ and has positive Taylor series at $\lambda = 0$. By a standard theorem in complex variables, the series converges at $\lambda = 1$, so $F^{-1} = A$ has nonnegative off-diagonal elements. $\square$

*Remarks* 1. This theorem is known as the Stieltjes-Ostrowski theorem after the work [107], [75]. Ostrowski's proof also depends upon the convergence of a Neumann series although his argument is more complicated.

***

# Page 66 (Journal Page 255)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 255

2. Since $(F^{-1})_{ij} = (\text{const})(\det F)^{1/2} \int x_i x_j e^{-x \cdot F x} d^n x$, this theorem is also a consequence of Theorem V.1, which, as we have seen, is in turn a consequence of Griffiths' first inequality for ferromagnets.

THEOREM A.2. *Let $A \in \mathcal{K}_n (n \le \infty)$. Choose a subset, $S$, of $m$ elements from $\{ i \mid 1 \le i < n + 1 \}$. Then the matrix $A_S = \{ A_{ij} \}_{i,j \in S}$ is in $\mathcal{K}_m$.*

*Proof.* Since $A$ is strictly positive definite and bounded, it follows that $A_S$ is strictly positive definite and bounded so that $A_S^{-1}$ exists and is positive definite. Corresponding to the decomposition of $\{ i \mid 1 \le i < n + 1 \}$ into $S$ and its complement, we can write:
$$ A = \begin{pmatrix} A_S & B \\ B^* & C \end{pmatrix} ; \quad A^{-1} = \begin{pmatrix} D & E \\ E^* & F \end{pmatrix} . $$
The equations $A_S D + B E^* = 1$ and $A_S E + B F = 0$ imply that $A_S^{-1} = D - E F^{-1} E^*$. But since $A^{-1} \in \mathcal{K}_n, E$ has nonpositive elements and $F \in \mathcal{M}_{n-m}$. By Theorem A.1, $F^{-1}$ has nonnegative elements so that $E F^{-1} E^*$ has nonnegative elements. It follows that the elements of $A_S^{-1}$ are less than the elements of $D$ which are, by hypothesis, nonpositive off-diagonal. $\square$

*Remark.* This proof also shows that $(A^{-1})_S - A_S^{-1}$ is positive semi-definite with nonnegative elements (cf. Theorem IV.7).

COROLLARY A.3. *If $A \in \mathcal{K}_n$, and $A_{ii} = 1$ for all $i$, then for any $i, j, k$*
$$ A_{ij} \ge A_{ik} A_{kj} . $$

*Proof.* Explicit computation shows that a positive definite matrix of the form
$$ \begin{pmatrix} 1 & a & b \\ a & 1 & c \\ b & c & 1 \end{pmatrix}^{-1} $$
has nonpositive off-diagonal elements if and only if $a \ge bc, b \ge ac, c \ge ab$. The corollary follows from Theorem A.2 upon taking $S = \{ i, j, k \}$. $\square$

## Appendix B. Correlation inequalities for the anharmonic oscillator: Alternate proofs

In this appendix, we wish to discuss alternate proofs of correlation inequalities for non-dimensional $P(\phi)$ theories, i.e., for the anharmonic oscillator (cf. (II.40)). Because $q(t)$ without any smearing is an $L^p$ function we can avoid the lattice approximation. It is also possible to demonstrate explicitly

***

# Page 67 (Journal Page 256)

256 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

that the usual ultraviolet cutoff, unlike the lattice cutoff, destroys the ferromagnetic property of the free theory. Since we have already given detailed proofs in Section V which establish the Griffiths inequalities for the anharmonic oscillator, we only sketch the ideas. On $L^2(\mathbf{R}, d\nu)$ where $d\nu(q) = \pi^{-1/2} e^{-q^2} dq$, the harmonic oscillator Hamiltonian ($m = 1$) is $H_0 = 1/2 (- (d^2/dq^2) + 2q(d/dq))$. We denote its vacuum (the function 1) by $\Omega_0$.

1. *Proof of G-I.* This proof avoids all the ideas of Markov field theory. Let $C$ be the cone of all $\psi(q)$ of the form $f(q) + q g(q)$ where $f$ and $g$ are positive even functions in $L^p(\mathbf{R}, d\nu)$ for all $p < \infty$. Then
(i) $\Omega_0 \in C$;
(ii) If $h_1, h_2 \in C$, then $\langle h_1, h_2 \rangle \ge 0$;
(iii) $q C \subset C$;
(iv) If $P(X) = \sum_{n=0}^{2m} a_n X^n$ with $a_{2m} > 0$ and $a_{2k+1} \le 0$ for $k = 0, \dots, m - 1$, then $e^{-P(q)} C \subset C$;
(v) $e^{-tH_0} C \subset C$;
(vi) $e^{-t(H_0+P(q))} C \subset C$ if $P$ is of the form given in (iv).
(i)-(iv) are easy to verify. (v) follows from Mehler's formula (II.80) and (vi) from (iv), (v) and the Trotter product formula. (i)-(iii) and (vi) imply that if $H = H_0 + P(q)$, then
$$ (\Omega_0, e^{-t_0 H} q^{n_1} \dots q^{n_r} e^{-t_r H} \Omega_0) \ge 0 , $$
which is the first Griffiths inequality.

2. *Proofs of G-I, II and FKG.* Let $t_1 < t_2 < \dots < t_n$ so that
$$ a_{ij} \equiv \langle q(t_i) q(t_j) \rangle = 1/2 e^{-|t_i - t_j|} $$
obeys
$$ a_{ij} = 2^{j-i-1} a_{i,i+1} a_{i+1,i+2} \dots a_{j-1,j} \quad \text{if } i < j . \eqno{\text{(B.1)}} $$
Using (B.1) it is easy to show by explicit computation that the inverse $\{b_{ij}\}$ of $\{a_{ij}\}$ is tri-diagonal with
$$ b_{11} = 2 D^{-1} \prod_{j \ne 1} (1 - \alpha_j^2) , $$
$$ b_{ii} = 2 D^{-1} (1 - \alpha_{i-1}^2 \alpha_i^2) \prod_{j \ne i,i-1} (1 - \alpha_j^2) , \quad i = 2, \dots, n - 1 , $$
$$ b_{nn} = 2 D^{-1} \prod_{j \ne n-1} (1 - \alpha_j^2) , $$
$$ b_{i,i+1} = b_{i+1,i} = -2 D^{-1} \alpha_i \prod_{j \ne i} (1 - \alpha_j^2) , \quad i = 1, \dots, n - 1 $$
where
$$ D = \prod_{j=1}^{n-1} (1 - \alpha_j^2) $$
and
$$ \alpha_i = \exp (-t_{i+1} + t_i) < 1 , $$
and the products have $j = 1, \dots, n - 1$.

***

# Page 68 (Journal Page 257)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 257

We thus see explicitly that $b$ has negative off-diagonal elements. If we now approximate $\int_a^b P(q(t)) dt$ in a Feynman-Kac formula by
$$ \sum_{j=1}^n \frac{(b - a)}{n} P(q(t_j)) $$
with $t_j = a + j(b - a)/n$, we can prove the correlation inequalities by the method of § V without use of the lattice approximation.

3. *Space Smearing Destroys "Ferromagnetism".* We claim that if $q(t)$ is replaced by $q_h(t) = \int h(t - s)q(s)ds$ the inverse of a correlation matrix $\langle q_h(t_i) q_h(t_j) \rangle$ ($i, j = 1, \dots, n$) will not be negative off-diagonal, at least for $h$ positive. For example if $h(t) = e^{-a|t|}$ with $a$ large, explicit computation shows that
$$ \langle q_h(0) q_h(t) \rangle < \langle q_h(0) q_h(t/2) \rangle \langle q_h(t/2) q_h(t) \rangle . \eqno{\text{(B.2)}} $$
Thus, by Corollary A.3, no covariance matrix
$$ \langle q_h(t_i) q_h(t_j) \rangle \quad \text{with } t_1 = 0, t_2 = t/2, t_3 = t; t_4, \dots, t_n \text{ arbitrary} $$
can have an inverse with negative off-diagonal elements.

4. *Remarks* 1. That the matrix $b$ in the proof of 2 above is tri-diagonal is a reflection of the locality of $-(d^2/dt^2) + 1$.
2. On an intuitive level, it is easy to understand why space smearing destroys "ferromagnetism". Heuristically, $(-(d^2/dt^2) + 1) \delta(t - t')$ has a positive infinity at $t = t'$ and negative infinities at $t = t' \pm \epsilon$. It is thus positive on-diagonal and negative off-diagonal. Smearing in $t$ tends to produce at least a small region $|t - t'| < \delta$ of positivity. This region includes some off-diagonal elements.

## Appendix C. Fisher convergence: Some technical results

Here we want to recall the definition of convergence in the sense of Fisher and van Hove [85] and to prove a few related technical facts of a geometric nature. Although all the results extend to $d$ dimensions, we restrict ourselves to $d = 2$. Given $a \in \mathbf{R}^+$, the lattice $a\mathbf{Z}^2$ induces in a natural way a decomposition of $\mathbf{R}^2$ into closed squares with side $a$ and centers in $a\mathbf{Z}^2$. Given $\Lambda \subset \mathbf{R}^2$ bounded we let $N_a^-(\Lambda)$ be the number of such squares inside $\Lambda$ and $N_a^+(\Lambda)$ be the number of such squares which intersect $\Lambda$. $|\Lambda|$ is the volume of $\Lambda$ and $d(\Lambda)$ its diameter. Finally
$$ V_a(\Lambda) = \{ x \in \mathbf{R}^2 \mid d(x, \Lambda) < a \} . \eqno{\text{(C.1)}} $$

*Definition.* Let $\Lambda_n$ be a family of regions of $\mathbf{R}^2$ with $|\Lambda_n| \to \infty$. We say that $\Lambda_n \to \infty$ in *van Hove* sense, if and only if for each $a \in \mathbf{R}^+$,

***

# Page 69 (Journal Page 258)

258 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

$N_a^-(\Lambda_n) / N_a^+(\Lambda_n) \to 1$ as $n \to \infty$. Our notion of Fisher convergence is slightly weaker than Ruelle's:

*Definition.* Let $\Lambda_n$ be a family of regions in $\mathbf{R}^2$ with $|\Lambda_n| \to \infty$. We say $\Lambda_n \to \infty$ in *Fisher* sense if and only if there is an $\epsilon > 0$ and a function $\pi$ on $(0, \epsilon)$ with $\lim_{\alpha \to 0} \pi(\alpha) = 0$ so that for each $\alpha \in (0, \epsilon)$, there is an $N$ so that
$$ V_{\alpha d(\Lambda_n)}(\Lambda_n) \le \pi(\alpha) |\Lambda_n| \eqno{\text{(C.2)}} $$
for all $n > N$.

Clearly, $|\Lambda| \le \pi d(\Lambda)^2$ and $V_a(\Lambda) \ge \pi a^2$ if $\Lambda \ne \emptyset$. Thus:

PROPOSITION C.1. *If $\Lambda_n \to \infty$ (Fisher), then there are constants $c_1, c_2, N$ such that for all $n > N$,*
$$ c_1 d(\Lambda_n)^2 \le |\Lambda_n| \le c_2 d(\Lambda_n)^2 . $$

It is also clear that $a^2 N_a^+(\Lambda) \ge |\Lambda|$ and that $a^2(N_a^+(\Lambda) - N_a^-(\Lambda)) \le V_{a\sqrt{2}}(\Lambda)$ so that:

PROPOSITION C.2. *If $\Lambda_n \to \infty$ (Fisher), then $\Lambda_n \to \infty$ (van Hove).*

The converse of this is false [85]; e.g., let $\Lambda_n$ be the rectangle of sides $n$ and $n^2$. In § VII.3, we used the following:

THEOREM C.3. *Let $\Lambda_n \to \infty$ (Fisher). Suppose $\eta' < 1$ is given. Then there exists $\Lambda'_n \subset \Lambda_n$ so that (Fig. C.1)*
(a) $\Lambda'_n \to \infty$ (Fisher);
(b) $|\Lambda'_n| / |\Lambda_n| \to 1$ as $n \to \infty$;
(c) $d(\Lambda'_n, \partial\Lambda_n) \ge d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}$.

[Diagram: FIGURE C.1 showing a region $\Lambda_n$ with a smaller subregion $\Lambda'_n$ at a minimum distance $d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}$ from the boundary $\partial\Lambda_n$.]

**FIGURE C.1**

*Proof.* Let $\Lambda'_n = \{ x \in \Lambda_n \mid d(x, \partial\Lambda_n) \ge d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'} \}$. Since $|\Lambda'_n| \le |\Lambda_n| \le |\Lambda'_n| + V_{d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}}(\Lambda'_n)$, (b) follows. (c) is trivial. Let $\epsilon' = \epsilon/2$ and $\pi'(\alpha) = 2\pi(2\alpha)$. Given $\alpha$, choose $N$ so that $n > N$ implies that (i) $|\Lambda'_n| \ge |\Lambda_n|/2$, (ii) $d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'} \le \alpha d(\Lambda_n)$, and (iii) $V_{2\alpha d(\Lambda_n)}(\Lambda_n) \le \pi(2\alpha) |\Lambda_n|$. Then, if $n > N$,
$$ V_{\alpha d(\Lambda'_n)}(\Lambda'_n) \le V_{\alpha d(\Lambda_n)}(\Lambda'_n) \le V_{\alpha d(\Lambda_n) + d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}}(\Lambda_n) \le V_{2\alpha d(\Lambda_n)}(\Lambda_n) \le 2\pi(2\alpha) |\Lambda'_n| . $$
This proves (a). $\square$

***

# Page 70 (Journal Page 259)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 259

Finally in § VI we used:

THEOREM C.4. *Suppose $\Lambda_n' \to \infty$ (Fisher). Let $\eta' < 1$ be given. Then we can find sets $\Lambda_n''$ and squares $\Lambda_n$ so that (Fig. C.2)*
(a) $\Lambda_n' \subset \Lambda_n, \Lambda_n'' \subset \Lambda_n, \Lambda_n' \cap \Lambda_n'' = \emptyset$;
(b) *for some $c > 0$ and all sufficiently large $n$, $c < |\Lambda_n'|/|\Lambda_n| < (1/2)$;*
(c) $(|\Lambda_n'| + |\Lambda_n''|) / |\Lambda_n| \to 1$ *as $n \to \infty$;*
(d) $\Lambda_n'' \to \infty$ (Fisher);
(e) $\text{dist }(\Lambda_n', \Lambda_n'') \ge d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}$ *for all $n$.*

[Diagram: FIGURE C.2 showing region $\Lambda_n'$ within a larger region $\Lambda_n''$ at a minimum distance $d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}$ from the common outer boundary.]

**FIGURE C.2**

*Proof.* Choose any square $\Lambda_n$ of side $3d(\Lambda_n')$ and center in $\Lambda_n'$. Let $\Lambda_n'' = \{ x \in \Lambda_n \setminus \Lambda_n' \mid d(x, \Lambda_n') \ge d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'} \}$. (a) and (e) are obvious. (b) follows from Proposition C.1 and its proof. (c) follows from
$$ |\Lambda_n'| + |\Lambda_n''| = |\Lambda_n| - |\Lambda_n \setminus (\Lambda_n' \cup \Lambda_n'')| \ge |\Lambda_n| - V_{d(\Lambda_n')^{\eta'}}(\partial\Lambda_n') $$
and the fact that $\Lambda_n' \to \infty$ (Fisher). Finally, to prove (d) we note that for all large $n$, $c_1 d(\Lambda_n) \le d(\Lambda_n'') \le c_2 d(\Lambda_n) (c_1, c_2 > 0)$ and that
$$ V_\alpha(\Lambda_n'') \le V_\alpha(\Lambda_n) + V_{\alpha+d(\Lambda_n)^{\eta'}}(\partial\Lambda_n') $$
since $\partial\Lambda_n'' \subset \partial\Lambda_n \cup \{ x \mid d(x, \partial\Lambda_n') = d(\Lambda_n')^{\eta'} \}$. (d) follows by mimicking the proof of Theorem C.3. $\square$

# Page 2 (Journal Page 191)

# The $P(\phi)_2$ Euclidean quantum field theory as classical statistical mechanics

By F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, and B. SIMON

Part I of this paper including the Bibliography appeared at the end of the preceding issue of this journal.

## Part II

## IV. Lattice Markov fields

To date in constructive field theory, two types of ultraviolet cutoffs have been used [32]:

1) A smeared field, i.e., $\phi(x)$ is replaced by $\phi_h(x) = \int h(x - y)\phi(y)dy$, where $h$ is some smooth positive approximation to the delta function;
2) Box and sharp momentum cutoff, i.e., $\phi(x)$ is replaced by the periodic field $\phi_{K,V}(x)$, which is obtained by approximating the Fourier integral defining $\phi(x)$ by a finite Fourier sum.

In this section we wish to introduce a new cutoff method in which $\mathbf{R}^d$ (space-imaginary time) is replaced by a lattice and the Laplacian $\Delta$ in the Euclidean propagator is approximated by a finite difference operator. The effect of this approximation is to replace the measure (II.25) by a perturbed Gaussian measure in a finite number of variables, $q_1, \dots, q_N$, each associated with a lattice point:

$$ e^{-U^{(\delta)}(g)} d\mu_0 \longrightarrow \text{const. } e^{-\sum_n P_n(q_n)} \exp \left( -\frac{1}{2} \vec{q} \cdot B \vec{q} \right) d^N q . \eqno{\text{(IV.1)}} $$

where the $P_n$ are semibounded polynomials and $B$ is a positive-definite $N \times N$ matrix with nonpositive off-diagonal entries. There are two main advantages to this approximation which play a key role in our proof of correlation inequalities (§ V): First, it is *locality preserving* in the sense that $U(g)$ is approximated by a sum $\sum P_n(q_n)$ in which each term involves the field $q_n$ at only a single lattice point. Secondly, the Gaussian exponential $e^{-1/2 \vec{q} \cdot B \vec{q}}$ provides a direct analogy with the *ferromagnetic* Ising model. Moreover the field theory on the lattice satisfies the Markov property.

Lattice cutoff fields have been previously discussed (but for time-zero Hamiltonian theories) by Wentzel [115] and Schiff [87].

***

# Page 3 (Journal Page 192)

192 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

IV.1. *The Lattice Approximation.* Although the lattice approximation for the free theory makes sense for an arbitrary number of dimensions, we shall take $d = 2$. Let $\delta > 0$ be the spacing parameter for the lattice $L_\delta = \{n\delta \mid n = (n_1, n_2) \in \mathbf{Z}^2\}$ in $\mathbf{R}^2$. Our definition of the lattice cutoff field $\phi_\delta(n)$ is motivated by the following observation of Nelson (private communication): If we view $\phi(x)$ as a continuum labelled (formal) family of Gaussian random variables, the joint covariance “matrix”, $\int \phi(x)\phi(y)d\mu_0$, is just the integral kernel of $(-\Delta + m^2)^{-1}$ (cf. (II.8)). Recall that in the formula for the joint density of a family of Gaussian random variables,

$$ f(q_1, \dots, q_N) = (2\pi)^{-N/2} |C|^{-1/2} \exp \left[ -\frac{1}{2} (\vec{q} - \vec{m}) \cdot C^{-1} (\vec{q} - \vec{m}) \right] , \eqno{\text{(IV.2)}} $$

it is the inverse of the covariance matrix, $C_{jk} = \text{Cov } (q_j, q_k)$, which appears in the exponent; here, $m_j = E(q_j)$ and $|C| = \det C$. Thus, formally, the free field measure is $e^{-1/2(\phi, A \phi)} d\phi$ where $A$ is the operator $(-\Delta + m^2)$. From a formal point of view, $-\Delta$ is positive on-diagonal and negative infinitesimally off-diagonal, as is evident from the finite difference approximation to $-\Delta$:

$$ (-\Delta_\delta f)(n\delta) = \delta^{-2} [4f(n\delta) - \sum_{|n'-n|=1} f(n'\delta)] \eqno{\text{(IV.3)}} $$

where we norm $\mathbf{Z}^2$ by $|(n_1, n_2)| = |n_1| + |n_2|$ so that the sum over $n' \in \mathbf{Z}^2$ takes place over the 4 nearest neighbours of $n$.

We now convert the above heuristic discussion to a precise one. Consider the Fourier transform from $l^2(\mathbf{Z}^2)$ to $L^2([-\pi/\delta, \pi/\delta]^2)$ defined by

$$ \hat{h}(k) = \frac{\delta}{2\pi} \sum_{n \in \mathbf{Z}^2} h(n) e^{-ik \cdot n \delta} . \eqno{\text{(IV.4)}} $$

If we regard $A = (-\Delta_\delta + m^2)$ as an operator on $l^2(\mathbf{Z}^2)$ by means of (IV.3), then we see it is a convolution operator $(Ah)(n) = \sum_{n'} a(n - n')h(n')$, where

$$ a(n) = \begin{cases} m^2 + 4\delta^{-2} & n = (0, 0) \\ -\delta^{-2} & n = (\pm 1, 0), (0, \pm 1) \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} . \end{cases} \eqno{\text{(IV.5)}} $$

Therefore, its image $\hat{A}$ on $L^2$ is multiplication by $(2\pi/\delta)\hat{a}(k)$; by a simple computation,

$$ (2\pi/\delta)\hat{a}(k) = \delta^{-2} (4 - 2 \cos(\delta k_1) - 2 \cos(\delta k_2)) + m^2 \equiv \mu_\delta(k)^2 . \eqno{\text{(IV.6)}} $$

We wish the cutoff field $\phi_\delta(n)$ to have covariance matrix $\delta^{-2} A^{-1}$; i.e.,

$$ \int \phi_\delta(n)\phi_\delta(n') d\mu_0 = (2\pi)^{-2} \int_\delta e^{ik \cdot (n - n') \delta} \mu_\delta(k)^{-2} d^2k , \eqno{\text{(IV.7)}} $$

***

# Page 4 (Journal Page 193)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 193

where $\int_\delta$ means that the range of integration is $|k_i| \le \pi/\delta$. Accordingly we define the lattice cutoff field $\phi_\delta(n)$ in terms of the field $\phi(x)$ of (II.22) by $\phi_\delta(n) = \phi(f_{\delta,n})$, where

$$ f_{\delta,n}(x) = (2\pi)^{-2} \int_\delta e^{ik \cdot (x - n\delta)} \mu(k)/\mu_\delta(k) d^2k . $$

Put differently, we have the first of the following definitions:

*Definitions.*

(i) $$ \phi_\delta(n) = (2\pi)^{-1} \int_\delta e^{-ik \cdot n\delta} [a^*(k) + a(-k)] \mu_\delta(k)^{-1} d^2k . \eqno{\text{(IV.8)}} $$

(ii) $:\phi_\delta^r(n): \equiv (2\pi)^{-r} \int_\delta e^{-i\delta n \cdot \sum k^{(j)}} \sum_{j=0}^r \binom{r}{j} a^*(k^{(1)}) \dots a^*(k^{(j)}) \cdot a(-k^{(j+1)}) \dots a(-k^{(r)}) \prod_i \mu_\delta(k_i)^{-1} dk_i$.

(iii) If $g \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$ then $\phi_\delta(g) = \sum_{n \in \mathbf{Z}^2} \delta^2 \phi_\delta(n) g(n\delta)$.

(iv) If $P$ is a polynomial and $g \in C_0^\infty$ we set $U(g) = \int g(x) :P(\phi(x)): dx \omega_0$, as in § II, and

$$ U_\delta(g) = \sum_n \delta^2 :P(\phi_\delta(n)): g(n\delta) \omega_0 . \eqno{\text{(IV.9)}} $$

The basic convergence result is:

THEOREM IV.1. *Let $P$ be a semibounded polynomial and let $g \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$ be nonnegative. Then, as $\delta \to 0$,*

$$ \phi_\delta(g) \longrightarrow \phi(g) , \eqno{\text{(IV.10a)}} $$
$$ U_\delta(g) \longrightarrow U(g) , \eqno{\text{(IV.10b)}} $$
$$ \exp(-U_\delta(g)) \longrightarrow \exp(-U(g)) , \eqno{\text{(IV.10c)}} $$

*where each of the above limits takes place in $L^p(Q, d\mu_0)$ for any $p < \infty$.*

The proof of the theorem depends on

LEMMA IV.2.

(i) *For each $k \in \mathbf{R}^2, \mu_\delta(k) \to \mu(k)$ as $\delta \to 0$.*
(ii) *If $|k_i| \le \pi/\delta, \mu_\delta(k)^{-1} \le (\pi/2) \mu(k)^{-1}$.*

*Proof.* From the definition (IV.6) we see that (i) is trivial. Clearly, (ii) is a consequence of the estimate

$$ 1 - \cos y \ge \frac{2}{\pi^2} y^2 \text{ if } y \in [-\pi, \pi] \eqno{\text{(IV.11)}} $$

which we prove as follows. Consider the $C^\infty$ function $F(y) = 1 - \cos y - 2y^2/\pi^2$ in the interval $[0, \pi]$. Note that $F(0) = F(\pi) = 0$, $F'(0) = 0$, and $F''(0) > 0$. Moreover $F''(y) = \cos y - 4/\pi^2$ has exactly one zero in $[0, \pi]$. Since $F''$ must vanish between zeros of $F'$ and since $F'(0) = 0$, $F'$ can vanish at most once in $(0, \pi)$. Therefore $F$ does not vanish in $(0, \pi)$. But $F(y) > 0$ for small $y$, and so $F(y) \ge 0$. $\square$

***

# Page 5 (Journal Page 194)

194 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

By Lemma IV.2(ii), Theorem IV.1 is a consequence of an extended conditioning convergence theorem (see the remark after Theorem II.23). We prefer a direct proof:

*Proof of Theorem IV.1.*
(a), (b) Since, for varying $\delta$, the vectors $U_\delta(g)$ have a fixed finite number of particles, it is sufficient by (II.23) to prove $L^2$-convergence. The $L^2$-convergence of the relevant kernels follows from Lemma IV.2 and the dominated convergence theorem.
(c) By mimicking the proof of Theorem II.10 that $e^{-U(g)} \in L^p$ and by using the uniform bound of Lemma IV.2(ii), we see that $\exp(-U_\delta(g))$ is uniformly bounded in $L^p$.
The convergence thus follows from (b), the estimate (II.24), and Hölder's inequality. $\square$

The lattice cutoff (smeared) Schwinger functions are defined by

$$ S_{g,\delta}(h_1, \dots, h_r) = \frac{\int \phi_\delta(h_1) \dots \phi_\delta(h_r) e^{-U_\delta(g)} d\mu_0}{\int e^{-U_\delta(g)} d\mu_0} \eqno{\text{(IV.12)}} $$

where $h_j \in C_0^\infty$. As an immediate consequence of Theorem IV.1, we deduce:

COROLLARY IV.3. *Let $h_1, \dots, h_r \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$. As $\delta \to 0$,*

$$ S_{g,\delta}(h_1, \dots, h_r) \longrightarrow S_g(h_1, \dots, h_r) . $$

IV.2. *Properties of the Lattice Theory.* In this section we investigate the properties of the lattice approximation, and in particular show that the measure reduces to the form (IV.1). To this end, note that in the expression (IV.12) for the lattice cutoff Schwinger function, only a finite number of Gaussian random variables are involved, namely the fields $q_n \equiv \phi_\delta(n) \equiv \phi(f_{\delta,n})$ for $n\delta \in \Lambda_\delta \equiv L_\delta \cap \Lambda$, where $\Lambda = \text{supp } g \cup \text{supp } h_1 \cup \dots \cup \text{supp } h_r$. Thus by (IV.2) and the definition of the measure $d\mu_0$, the numerator in (IV.12) reduces to a sum of terms of the form

$$ (2\pi)^{-N/2} |C|^{-1/2} \int q_{n_1} \dots q_{n_r} \exp \left[ -\sum_n \delta^2 :P(q_n): g(n\delta) \right] \eqno{\text{(IV.13)}} $$
$$ \cdot \exp \left( -\frac{1}{2} \vec{q} \cdot C^{-1} \vec{q} \right) d^N q , $$

where $N = |\Lambda_\delta|$, the number of points in $\Lambda_\delta$, and $C$ is the $N \times N$ covariance matrix of the $q$’s. The same conclusion holds for the denominator of (IV.12), and, more generally, for any expectation

$$ \frac{\int F(\phi_\delta(n_1), \dots, \phi_\delta(n_r)) e^{-U_\delta(g)} d\mu_0}{\int e^{-U_\delta(g)} d\mu_0} \eqno{\text{(IV.14)}} $$

***

# Page 6 (Journal Page 195)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 195

where $F$ is a function on $\mathbf{R}^r$.
Now we constructed the lattice cutoff field so that its covariance matrix

$$ C_{nn'} = \int \phi_\delta(n)\phi_\delta(n') d\mu_0 \eqno{\text{(IV.15)}} $$

gives rise to an operator $C$ on $l^2$ whose inverse $C^{-1} = \delta^2 A$ has particularly simple matrix elements (see (IV.5)). In fact, by (IV.5) and (IV.6), $A \in \mathcal{M}_\infty$ and $C \in \mathcal{K}_\infty$ where we define:

*Definitions.* Let $\mathcal{M}_\infty$ denote the class of positive invertible operators on $l^2$ with nonpositive off-diagonal entries. Let $\mathcal{K}_\infty = \{C \mid C^{-1} \in \mathcal{M}_\infty\}$.

These classes of infinite matrices have rather interesting properties some of which are established in Appendix A. In particular, Theorem A.2 shows that any *finite* submatrix of $C \in \mathcal{K}_\infty$ retains the property that its inverse is a positive definite matrix with nonpositive off-diagonal entries. We summarize these facts about $C$:

THEOREM IV.4.
(i) *The covariance matrix (IV.15) defines a bounded, positive, invertible operator $C$ on $l^2(\mathbf{Z}^2)$.*
(ii) $C^{-1} = \delta^2 A$ *has nonpositive off-diagonal matrix elements (see (IV.5)); i.e., $C \in \mathcal{K}_\infty$.*
(iii) *Let $S$ be a finite subset of $\mathbf{Z}^2$ and let $D$ be the matrix $(C_{nn'})_{n,n' \in S}$. Then $D$ is strictly positive-definite and $D^{-1}$ is a positive-definite matrix with nonpositive off-diagonal entries.*

If for a fixed $\delta > 0$ we are dealing only with the lattice fields in a bounded region $\Lambda$, let us denote the (finite) covariance matrix of these fields by $C^\Lambda$ (and similarly for submatrices of the operator $A$). According to the above discussion, Theorem IV.4(iii) shows that in expectations of the form (IV.14) the measure reduces to the form (IV.1) where $B = (C^\Lambda)^{-1}$ is a positive-definite matrix with nonpositive off-diagonal entries. Although this conclusion is sufficient for the purposes of the next section, we can go further in determining $(C^\Lambda)^{-1}$. Of course, $(C^\Lambda)^{-1} \ne \delta^2 A^\Lambda$; but, as we show in Theorem IV.7 below,

$$ \delta^{-2}(C^\Lambda)^{-1} = A^\Lambda - B_{\partial\Lambda} \eqno{\text{(IV.16)}} $$

where the matrix $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ is “concentrated on the boundary of $\Lambda_\delta$”. By this we mean the following: Given a set $\Lambda \subset \mathbf{R}^2$ with its enclosed lattice points $\Lambda_\delta$, we make these definitions:

*Definitions.*
(i) $\Lambda_\delta^{ext} = L_\delta \setminus \Lambda_\delta$.
(ii) $\Lambda_\delta^{int} = \{n\delta \in \Lambda_\delta \mid m\delta \in \Lambda_\delta \text{ if } |m - n| = 1\}$.

***

# Page 7 (Journal Page 196)

196 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

(iii) The boundary $\partial\Lambda_\delta = \Lambda_\delta \setminus \Lambda_\delta^{int}$.
(iv) $P_\Lambda$ is the projection in $l^2(\mathbf{Z}^2)$ onto sequences $a$ such that $a_n = 0$ if $n\delta \notin \Lambda$.

We say that an operator $B$ is concentrated on the set $\Lambda$ if $B = P_\Lambda B P_\Lambda$, and similarly for a matrix $B$, where we identify $P_\Lambda$ with its restriction to the finite-dimensional subspace of $l^2$ on which the matrix acts. In addition we identify $C^\Lambda$ with $P_\Lambda C P_\Lambda$.

The relation (IV.16) turns out to be connected with the lattice Markov property (see Theorem IV.8 below) and will be useful in understanding the results of the following sections. The basic idea in the proof of (IV.16) is that $(C^\Lambda)^{-1}$ can be calculated from

$$ \delta^2 C^\Lambda = \lim_{R \to \infty} P_\Lambda (A^R)^{-1} P_\Lambda . \eqno{\text{(IV.17)}} $$

To prove (IV.17) we first note:

LEMMA IV.5. *For some $\alpha > 0, C_{nn'} = O(e^{-\alpha|n-n'|})$.*

*Proof.* It is sufficient to show that $C_{n0}$ decreases exponentially in $n_1 > 0$, uniformly in $n_2$. From (IV.7) and the definition (IV.15),

$$ C_{n0} = (2\pi)^{-2} \int_{-\pi/\delta}^{\pi/\delta} dk_2 e^{ik_2 n_2 \delta} \int_{-\pi/\delta}^{\pi/\delta} dk_1 e^{ik_1 n_1 \delta} \mu_\delta(k)^{-2} $$

For fixed $k_2, \mu_\delta(k)^{-2}$ is an analytic function of $k_1$ in the strip $-2\pi/\delta < \text{Re } k_1 < 2\pi/\delta$ except at the two zeros of $\mu_\delta(k) = 0$, i.e., at $k_1 = \pm i \kappa(k_2)$, where by an elementary computation, $\kappa \ge \delta^{-1} \log(1 + m^2 \delta^2)$. Therefore by the Cauchy integral theorem, the integral in $k_1$ along the line segment $[-\pi/\delta, \pi/\delta]$ is the same as the integral along $[(-\pi + i a)/\delta, (\pi + i a)/\delta]$ for any $0 < a < \kappa$, since the integrals over the other two sides of the rectangle cancel by periodicity. Thus the integral over $k_1$ is $O(e^{-a n_1})$, uniformly in $k_2$. $\square$

LEMMA IV.6. *If $\Lambda \subset \mathbf{R}^2$ is a fixed bounded region then, as the regions $R$ go to infinity, (IV.17) holds.*

*Proof.* Define the operator $B = P_R A P_{R'} + P_{R'} A P_R$, where $R'$ is the complement of $R$. Then we have $A - B = A^R \oplus A^{R'}$. From (IV.6) we note the bounds $m^2 \le A \le 8\delta^{-2} + m^2$, so that $A, A^R, A^{R'}$ and $B$ are bounded and the first three have bounded inverses (all bounds uniform in $R$). Therefore

$$ (A - B)^{-1} = (A^R)^{-1} \oplus (A^{R'})^{-1} . $$

Assuming that $\Lambda \subset R$ we have $P_\Lambda (A^{R'})^{-1} = 0$ and so

$$ P_\Lambda (A^R)^{-1} P_\Lambda = P_\Lambda (A - B)^{-1} P_\Lambda = P_\Lambda (A^{-1} + (A - B)^{-1} B A^{-1}) P_\Lambda $$
$$ = \delta^2 C^\Lambda + \delta^2 P_\Lambda (A^R)^{-1} B C P_\Lambda . $$

***

# Page 8 (Journal Page 197)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 197

But since $A$ has matrix elements only between nearest neighbours, $B$ is concentrated on the set $R_0 = \partial R_\delta \cup \partial R'_\delta$. From the previous lemma and the uniform bounds on $(A^R)^{-1}$ and $B$ we thus conclude that

$$ P_\Lambda (A^R)^{-1} P_\Lambda = \delta^2 C^\Lambda + O(\exp[-\alpha \text{ dist}(R_0, \Lambda)]) $$

and (IV.17) follows. $\square$

We then obtain this improvement of Theorem IV.4 (iii):

THEOREM IV.7. *If $\Lambda \subset \mathbf{R}^2$ is a bounded region, then $(C^\Lambda)^{-1} = \delta^2(A^\Lambda - B_{\partial\Lambda})$, where $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ is a positive semi-definite matrix with nonnegative elements that is concentrated on the boundary $\partial\Lambda_\delta$.*

*Proof.* Let $M = |\Lambda_\delta|$ and $N = |R_\delta|$. We determine $(C^\Lambda)^{-1}$ by formula (IV.2), i.e., as the matrix in the exponential of the density function for the Gaussian random variables on the lattice $\Lambda_\delta$:

$$ C_{nn'}^\Lambda = (2\pi)^{-M/2} |C^\Lambda|^{-1/2} \int q_n q_{n'} e^{-1/2 q \cdot (C^\Lambda)^{-1} q} d^M q , \eqno{\text{(IV.18)}} $$

where $n\delta, n'\delta \in \Lambda_\delta$. On the other hand by Lemma IV.6 we also have

$$ \delta^2 C_{nn'}^\Lambda = \lim_{R \to \infty} (2\pi)^{-N/2} |A^R|^{1/2} \int q_n q_{n'} e^{-1/2 q^R \cdot A^R q^R} d^N q , \eqno{\text{(IV.19)}} $$

where we have again used (IV.2). Now suppose that for a fixed $R \supset \Lambda$ we integrate out the variables in $R \setminus \Lambda$ in the integral in (IV.19). Denote the resulting matrix in the Gaussian by $A^R_\Lambda$, i.e.,

$$ (2\pi)^{-N/2} |A^R|^{1/2} \int e^{-1/2 q^R \cdot A^R q^R} d q^{R \setminus \Lambda} = (2\pi)^{-M/2} |A_\Lambda^R|^{1/2} e^{-1/2 q^\Lambda \cdot A_\Lambda^R q^\Lambda} \eqno{\text{(IV.20)}} $$

where $q^R$ stands for the variables on $R_\delta$. It is obvious from the definition (IV.20) that $A_\Lambda^R$ is a positive definite matrix with

$$ \inf \sigma(A_\Lambda^R) \ge \inf \sigma(A^R) . \eqno{\text{(IV.21)}} $$

We claim that the matrix $B_{\partial\Lambda}^R = A^\Lambda - A_\Lambda^R$ is a positive semi-definite matrix with nonnegative elements that is concentrated on the boundary $\partial\Lambda_\delta$. Since we are assured by Lemma IV.6 that the limit in (IV.19) exists for all $n, n' \in \delta^{-1}\Lambda_\delta$, we see from (IV.18)-(IV.20) that these properties of $B_{\partial\Lambda}^R$ yield the theorem. The claim is proved by induction on the number of variables in $R \setminus \Lambda$. What is the effect of integrating out a single variable $q$ in a Gaussian integral? If the terms involving $q$ are of the form $\exp(-(c/2)q^2 - q \sum a_n q_n)$, then integrating out $q$ leaves a factor const. $\exp[1/2c (\sum a_n q_n)^2]$. Thus as each variable in $R \setminus \Lambda$ is integrated out, more couplings are introduced for the remaining variables. However, since $A^R$ couples only nearest neighbours, the variables in $\Lambda_\delta^{int}$ are not coupled to the variables in $\Lambda_\delta^{ext}$, and integrating

***

# Page 9 (Journal Page 198)

198 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

out variables in $\Lambda_\delta^{ext}$ cannot affect the terms involving variables in $\Lambda_\delta^{int}$. Clearly, then, $B_{\partial\Lambda}^R$ is always concentrated on $\partial\Lambda_\delta$. As for the sign of $B_{\partial\Lambda}^R$, note that each integration modifies the positive-definite quadratic form in the remaining variables by subtracting a term of the form $1/2c (\sum a_n q_n)^2$ in which, we claim, all the coefficients are nonnegative. The reason is that $c$ is always positive, since by (IV.21) the quadratic form remains positive-definite after each integration, and the $a_n$’s always have the same sign, namely, negative. For, from the definition (IV.5) of $A^R$, the couplings start out nonpositive and each integration only decreases them. $\square$

*Example.* To illustrate the theorem, we can explicitly calculate $(C^\Lambda)^{-1}$ and $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ when $d = 1$. In one dimension, $A = (-\Delta_\delta + m^2)$ has matrix elements $A_{nn} = m^2 + 2\delta^{-2} \equiv a_0$ when $n = n'$, $A_{nn'} = -\delta^{-2} \equiv -a_1$ when $n = n' \pm 1$, and $A_{nn'} = 0$ otherwise. In momentum space $\hat{A}$ is multiplication by $\mu_\delta(k)^2 = m^2 + 2\delta^{-2}(1 - \cos k\delta)$. The matrix elements of the covariance operator, $C_{nn'} = (2\pi)^{-1} \int e^{ik\delta(n-n')} \mu_\delta(k)^{-2} dk$, can be evaluated by contour integration and we find

$$ C_{nn'} = a_1^{-1} x^{|n-n'|+1}/(1 - x^2) \eqno{\text{(IV.22)}} $$

where $x$ is the solution of $a_1 x^2 - a_0 x + a_1 = 0$ in $0 < x < 1$, i.e., $x = [(1 + (m\delta/2)^2)^{1/2} - m\delta/2]^2$. Note that $C_{nn'}$ decreases exponentially with $|n - n'|$.

Let $\Lambda = (0, M\delta]$ so that $\Lambda_\delta = \{\delta, 2\delta, \dots, M\delta\}$ and $\partial\Lambda_\delta = \{\delta, M\delta\}$. From the definition (IV.22) we can readily compute $(C^\Lambda)^{-1}$, and we obtain

$$ (C^\Lambda)^{-1} = \frac{1}{x\delta} \begin{pmatrix} 1 & -x & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ -x & 1+x^2 & -x & 0 & \dots \\ 0 & -x & 1+x^2 & -x & \\ & & \dots & -x & \\ \dots & \dots & 0 & -x & 1 \end{pmatrix} . $$

Thus we see that $\delta^{-2}(C^\Lambda)^{-1} = A^\Lambda - B_{\partial\Lambda}$, where

$$ B_{\partial\Lambda} = \begin{pmatrix} a_1 x & 0 & \dots \\ 0 & 0 & & \vdots \\ \vdots & & 0 & 0 \\ \dots & \dots & 0 & a_1 x \end{pmatrix} . $$

*Remarks* 1. The main point of Theorem IV.7 is that even in a bounded region, the measure still couples only nearest neighbours, except for the boundary variables.

***

# Page 10 (Journal Page 199)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 199

2. The role of the boundary matrix $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ is to adjust the boundary condition associated with the (finite difference) Laplacian. Thus, if to the $M = |\Lambda_\delta|$ Gaussian random variables on the lattice $\Lambda_\delta$ we associate the measure const. $e^{-1/2 q \cdot (C^\Lambda)^{-1} q} d^M q$, we are taking “free” boundary conditions; on the other hand, it is heuristically reasonable that the measure const. $e^{-1/2 \delta^2 q \cdot A^\Lambda q} dq$ gives Dirichlet B.C. since the effect of the $A^\Lambda$ is to ignore the variables just outside $\Lambda_\delta$ in the definition of the finite difference approximation to $-\Delta$. We return to this question and the related convergence theorem in § IV.3. Similarly we expect that arbitrary B.C. may be obtained by an appropriate choice of boundary matrix.

3. The assertions about the sign and semi-definiteness of $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ also follow from the method of Appendix A (see the remark after Theorem A.2). The semi-definiteness, which can be expressed as $\delta^2(A^\Lambda)^{-1} \le C^\Lambda$, is the lattice version of the positive-definiteness relationship between Dirichlet and free B.C. noted in § II. The significance of the sign of $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ will become clear in § V when we show by correlation inequalities that the free Schwinger functions are greater than the Dirichlet Schwinger functions.

4. The fact that the influence of the variables in $\Lambda^{ext}$ can be felt by the variables in $\Lambda$ only on $\partial\Lambda$ is of course an expression of the Markov property of the free field theory. In § VII we return to this idea of boundary terms in our discussion of equilibrium equations.

Finally, we discuss the Markov property for the lattice theory. In the continuous case the Markov property amounts to a statement of the fact that $(-\Delta + m^2)$ is a local operator (see the proof of Proposition II.3). In the lattice case the corresponding fact is that the finite difference operator $A = (-\Delta_\delta + m^2)$ couples nearest neighbours only. An abstract discussion of the lattice theory and its Markov property proceeds just as in § II.1. In fact the lattice theory is just a *sub-theory* of the continuous one: The single-particle Hilbert space $N_\delta$ consists of sequences $a$ on the lattice $L_\delta$ with inner product

$$ \langle a, b \rangle_\delta = \sum_{n,n'} \bar{a}_n C_{nn'} b_{n'} . \eqno{\text{(IV.23)}} $$

If we identify $a$ with the function $f(x) = \sum_n a_n f_{\delta,n}(x)$ we have an isometric imbedding $N_\delta \subset N$. On the associated $Q$-space, $Q_\delta \equiv Q_{N_\delta}$, the Markov property takes the usual form: We define $e_{R_\delta}$ to be the projection in $N_\delta$ onto sequences supported in $R_\delta$, and $E_{R_\delta}$ to be $\Gamma(e_{R_\delta})$. Then

THEOREM IV.8. *If $R$ and $S$ are closed disjoint regions in $\mathbf{R}^2$, then*

$$ E_{R_\delta} E_{S_\delta} = E_{\partial R_\delta} E_{S_\delta} . \eqno{\text{(IV.24)}} $$

***

# Page 11 (Journal Page 200)

200 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

*Proof 1.* The proof of (IV.24) reduces to the corresponding single particle relation which we verify as in Proposition II.3: For any $a \in \text{Ran } e_{R_\delta}$, we must show that $e_{R_\delta} a$ is supported on $\partial R_\delta$, or equivalently that $\sum_n \overline{(e_{R_\delta} a)_n} b_n = 0$ for all finite sequences $b$ supported in $R^{int}$. But

$$ \sum_n \overline{(e_{R_\delta} a)_n} b_n = \langle e_{R_\delta} a, Ab \rangle_\delta = \langle a, e_{R_\delta} Ab \rangle_\delta = \langle a, Ab \rangle_\delta = \sum \bar{a}_n b_n = 0 $$

where $e_{R_\delta} Ab = Ab$ since $A$ couples only nearest neighbours. $\square$

*Proof 2.* But a more concrete version of (IV.24) is at hand (at least for bounded regions) since the lattice approximation has provided a concrete representation of $Q_\delta$. Thus if $F$ is a function of the random variables $q^S$ on $S_\delta$, the projection $E_{R_\delta} F$ is defined by the identity

$$ (2\pi)^{-|R_\delta|/2} |C^R|^{-1/2} \int G(q^R) (E_{R_\delta} F)(q^R) e^{-1/2 q^R \cdot (C^R)^{-1} q^R} d q^R $$
$$ = (2\pi)^{-|R_\delta \cup S_\delta|/2} |C^{R \cup S}|^{-1/2} \int G(q^R) F(q^S) e^{-1/2 q^{R \cup S} \cdot (C^{R \cup S})^{-1} q^{R \cup S}} d q^{R \cup S} , $$

for all $G \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^{|R_\delta|})$. But by Theorem IV.7, $(C^{R \cup S})^{-1}$ does not couple any of the variables in $R^{int}$ to variables in $S_\delta$. Therefore, upon integrating over $dq^S$ on the right hand side, we see that $E_{R_\delta} F$ is a function only of the variables in $\partial R_\delta$. $\square$

As for the interacting theory, it is clear from (IV.13) that the interaction does not destroy the Markov property since it does not couple different $q$’s; i.e., the interaction is local:

$$ U_{\Lambda_\delta} = \sum_{n\delta \in \Lambda_\delta} \delta^2 :P_n(q_n): , \eqno{\text{(IV.25)}} $$

where the sum takes place over sites $n\delta \in \Lambda_\delta$ ($\Lambda = \text{supp } g$).

In Theorem IV.8 we were of course considering the lattice theory with free boundary conditions, i.e., the covariance in the region $\Lambda$ is given by $C^\Lambda$ where $(C^\Lambda)^{-1} = \delta^2(A^\Lambda - B_{\partial\Lambda})$, as in Theorem IV.7. However, it is obvious from the second proof of Theorem IV.8 that the Markov property depends only on the nearest neighbour form of the Gaussian density; hence the Markov property will hold for arbitrary boundary conditions; i.e., if the inverse of the covariance matrix has the form $\delta^2(A^\Lambda - B_{\partial\Lambda})$ where $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ is an arbitrary matrix concentrated on $\partial\Lambda_\delta$. Given the lattice $L_\delta$, these observations lead us to:

*Definition.* In two Euclidean space dimensions, the *polynomially inter-*
This is a 1-1 transcription of **Part II** of the paper, continuing from journal page 201 through 210 (Screenshots 12 through 21).

***

# Page 12 (Journal Page 201)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 201

*acting lattice Markov field theory with boundary condition* $\{B_{\partial\Lambda}\}$ is defined in the bounded region $\Lambda \subset \mathbf{R}^2$ by the following measure for the fields $q_n$ on the lattice $\Lambda_\delta$:

$$ d\nu_\Lambda(q) = \frac{\exp(-U_{\Lambda_\delta} - \frac{1}{2} q \cdot B q) d^N q}{\int \exp(-U_{\Lambda_\delta} - \frac{1}{2} q \cdot B q) d^N q} ; \eqno{\text{(IV.26)}} $$

here $U_{\Lambda_\delta}$ is defined by (IV.25) in terms of the given semi-bounded polynomials $P_n, N = |\Lambda_\delta|$, and $B$ is the $N \times N$ positive definite matrix, $B = \delta^2(A^\Lambda - B_{\partial\Lambda})$ where $A^\Lambda$ is the matrix of the operator (IV.5) restricted to $\Lambda_\delta$ and $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ is concentrated on $\partial\Lambda_\delta$.

In closing this section we remark that one can prove $L^p$-estimates (e.g., hypercontractivity, checkerboard = pegboard) for the lattice theory, just as in § III. The hypercontractivity of projections is based on a calculation similar to (IV.22).

IV.3. *Dirichlet Boundary Conditions.* We now consider the lattice theory with Dirichlet B.C. in more detail. Our main goal is to verify the claim made in Remark 2 following Theorem IV.7 that the lattice measure const. $e^{-(1/2)\delta^2 q \cdot A^\Lambda q} dq$ corresponds to Dirichlet B.C. on the boundary of $\Lambda$. Throughout this discussion the region $\Lambda$ will be fixed, and we shall generally neglect attaching a subscript $\Lambda$ to $\Lambda$-dependent objects.

As in the continuum case the Dirichlet field $\phi_\delta^D$ is obtained by “conditioning on $\Lambda_\delta^{ext}$”. Thus let $P_\delta$ be the projection orthogonal in $N_\delta$ (cf. (IV.23)) onto sequences supported in $\Lambda_\delta^{ext}$. We write $\phi_\delta(a) = \sum a_n \phi_\delta(n)$ if $a \in l^2(\mathbf{Z}^2)$ and we let $e^{(n)}$ be the vector in $l^2$ with components $e_m^{(n)} = \delta_{nm}$. Then we have:

*Definition.* On the lattice $L_\delta$, the Dirichlet field in the region $\Lambda$ is given by
$$ \phi_\delta^D(n) = \phi_\delta((I - P_\delta)e^{(n)}) . \eqno{\text{(IV.27)}} $$
Note that if $n\delta \notin \Lambda$, then $\phi_\delta^D(n) \equiv 0$, and that if $n\delta \in \Lambda$,
$$ \phi_\delta^D(n) = \phi_\delta(n) - \sum_m \alpha_m^{(n)} \phi_\delta(m) , \eqno{\text{(IV.28)}} $$
where by the Markov property the vector $\alpha^{(n)} = P_\delta e^{(n)}$ has nonzero components $\alpha_m^{(n)}$ only if $m\delta \in \partial(\Lambda')_\delta$, where $\Lambda' = \mathbf{R}^2 \setminus \Lambda$. The interaction $U_\delta^D(g)$ is defined as in (IV.9) with the replacement of $\phi_\delta(n)$ by $\phi_\delta^D(n)$. Notice that since $\phi_\delta^D$ vanishes outside $\Lambda$, only the values of $g$ in $\Lambda$ matter.

First we verify that the covariance matrix of the Dirichlet fields
$$ C_{jk}^{\Lambda,D} = \int \phi_\delta^D(j)\phi_\delta^D(k) d\mu_0 , \eqno{\text{(IV.29)}} $$

***

# Page 13 (Journal Page 202)

202 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

has boundary matrix $B_{\partial\Lambda} = 0$:

THEOREM IV.9. *Fix $\delta > 0$ and $\Lambda$ a bounded region in $\mathbf{R}^2$. Then as $|\Lambda_\delta| \times |\Lambda_\delta|$ matrices,*
$$ (C^{\Lambda,D})^{-1} = \delta^2 A^\Lambda . \eqno{\text{(IV.30)}} $$

*Proof.* From the definitions (IV.29), (IV.27) and (IV.23)
$$ \begin{aligned} C_{jk}^{\Lambda,D} &= \langle e^{(j)}, (I - P_\delta)e^{(k)} \rangle_\delta \\ &= C_{jk} - \sum_m C_{jm} \alpha_m^{(k)} . \end{aligned} $$
This equality holds true even if $j\delta \notin \Lambda$ in which case the last expression vanishes; thus if $k, i \in \Lambda/\delta$,
$$ \begin{aligned} \delta^2 \sum_{j \in \Lambda/\delta} A_{ij} C_{jk}^{\Lambda,D} &= \delta^2 \sum_{j \in \mathbf{Z}^2} A_{ij} (C_{jk} - \sum_m C_{jm} \alpha_m^{(k)}) \\ &= \delta_{ik} - \sum_m \delta_{im} \alpha_m^{(k)} \\ &= \delta_{ik} \end{aligned} $$
since the sum over $m$ is over $m\delta \in \partial(\Lambda')_\delta$, which is disjoint from $\Lambda_\delta$. $\square$

Secondly, we establish the convergence of the lattice Dirichlet theories (both “full” and “half”) to the continuum Dirichlet theories in the same region. However, it is necessary to impose a regularity condition on $\Lambda$, as is shown by the following.

*Example.* Let $\Lambda_1$ be the unit disk and let $\Lambda_2 = \Lambda_1 \setminus S$ where $S$ is the line segment $\{(x, \pi x) \mid x > 0\}$. Obviously $(\Lambda_1)_\delta = (\Lambda_2)_\delta$ if $\delta$ is rational, in which case the lattice Dirichlet theories agree for the two regions. On the other hand, there are elements in the one-particle space $N$ of (II.6) which are supported in $\Lambda_1 \setminus \Lambda_2$ so that the continuum Dirichlet theories differ for the two regions.

*Definition.* Let $\Lambda \subset \mathbf{R}^2$ be open and bounded and let $\Lambda'$ be its complement. We say $\Lambda$ is *regular* if and only if any distribution $f \in N$ with support in $\Lambda'$ is a limit (in $N$) of elements in $C_0^\infty(\Lambda')$.

*Remark.* By scaling and convolution it is easy to show that $\Lambda$ is regular if it is convex.

We now state the convergence theorem for the *full* Dirichlet theory:

THEOREM IV.10. *Let $\Lambda$ be an open, bounded, regular region in $\mathbf{R}^2$. Let $g \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$ be nonnegative. Let $\phi_\delta^D$ ($\phi^D$) denote the Dirichlet field on the lattice $L_\delta$ (respectively, continuum Dirichlet field), both in the region $\Lambda$, and let $U_\delta^D(g)$ (resp. $U^D(g)$) be the corresponding interaction defined above (resp. in (II.63)) in terms of a fixed semibounded polynomial. Then, as $\delta \to 0$,*

***

# Page 14 (Journal Page 203)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 203

$$ \phi_\delta^D(g) \longrightarrow \phi^D(g) , \eqno{\text{(IV.31a)}} $$
$$ U_\delta^D(g) \longrightarrow U^D(g) , \eqno{\text{(IV.31b)}} $$
$$ \exp(-U_\delta^D(g)) \longrightarrow \exp(-U^D(g)) \eqno{\text{(IV.31c)}} $$
*where each of the above limits takes place in $L^p(Q, d\mu_0)$ for any $p < \infty$.*

*Remark.* $g$ need not have support in $\Lambda$; moreover only the values of $g$ in $\Lambda$ matter. Thus, for example, we can replace $g$ with $\chi_\Lambda$, the characteristic function of $\Lambda$.

The proof of the theorem depends on

LEMMA IV.11. *Let $\Lambda$ be an open, bounded regular region in $\mathbf{R}^2$, and let $P$ be the orthogonal projection in $N$ onto the distributions with support in $\Lambda'$. Then*
$$ s\text{-lim}_{\delta \to 0} P_\delta = P . \eqno{\text{(IV.32)}} $$

*Remark.* By the isometric imbedding of $N_\delta$ in $N$, we regard $P_\delta$ as an operator on $N$; i.e., $P_\delta$ is the projection onto the subspace of $N$ spanned by $\{f_{\delta,n} \mid n\delta \notin \Lambda\}$.

*Proof.* Given $g \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$, define $g_\delta(x) = \delta^2 \sum_n g(n\delta)f_{\delta,n}(x)$. Then it is easy to see by Lemma IV.2 and the dominated convergence theorem that $g_\delta \to g$ and
$$ (-\Delta_\delta + m^2)g_\delta \longrightarrow (-\Delta + m^2)g \eqno{\text{(IV.33)}} $$
in $N$. Suppose that $g \in C_0^\infty(\Lambda')$. Then $g_\delta \in \text{Ran } P_\delta$, so that by Bessel's inequality $\|P_\delta g - g\| \le \|g_\delta - g\| \to 0$ as $\delta \to 0$. Since $\Lambda$ is regular, we conclude that
$$ P_\delta g \longrightarrow g \text{ if } g \in \text{Ran } P . \eqno{\text{(IV.34)}} $$
Now suppose that $g \in N$. Since the ball of radius $\|g\|$ in $N$ is weakly compact we know that $\{P_\delta g\}$ has a weakly convergent subsequence. Let $f$ be any weak limit point of $\{P_\delta g\}$. We claim that to prove (IV.32) it is sufficient to show that $f = Pg$, for then $P_\delta g \to Pg$ weakly and hence strongly since $P_\delta$ and $P$ are projections. To this end, let $h \in C_0^\infty(\Lambda)$ be arbitrary. Since $\text{supp } h \cap \partial(\Lambda')_\delta = \emptyset, \langle (-\Delta_\delta + m^2)h_\delta, P_\delta g \rangle_N = 0$. Thus by (IV.33), $\langle (-\Delta + m^2)h, f \rangle_N = \int h(x) \overline{f(x)} dx = 0$ so that $f \in \text{Ran } P$. Now let $h$ be arbitrary in $N$. Then $(h, f) = (Ph, f) = \lim_\delta(Ph, P_\delta g) = (Ph, g)$ by (IV.34). We conclude that $f = Pg$. $\square$

*Proof of Theorem IV.10.*
(a), (b) By the general theory of conditioning (cf. § II.5), $U^D(g) = \Gamma(I - P)U(g)$ and $U_\delta^D(g) = \Gamma(I - P_\delta)U_\delta(g)$. Clearly $L^2$-convergence follows from Theorem IV.1 and Lemma IV.11, and $L^p$-convergence from (II.23).

***

# Page 14 (Journal Page 204)

204 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

(c) By (II.24) we need only show that $\sup_\delta \|\exp(-U_\delta^D(g))\|_p$ is finite for each fixed $p < \infty$. But by Corollary II.21,
$$ \|\exp(-U_\delta^D(g))\|_p \le \|\exp(-U_\delta(g))\|_p $$
so that the uniform $L^p$ bound follows from the proof of Theorem IV.1. $\square$

Theorems IV.9 and IV.10 together justify our conclusion that the choice of boundary matrix $B_{\partial\Lambda} = 0$ gives Dirichlet B.C.

The lattice cutoff (smeared) Schwinger functions with Dirichlet B.C. in the region $\Lambda$ are defined by
$$ S_{\Lambda,\delta}^D(h_1, \dots, h_r) = \frac{\int \phi_\delta^D(h_1) \dots \phi_\delta^D(h_r) e^{-U_\delta^D(\chi_\Lambda)} d\mu_0}{\int e^{-U_\delta^D(\chi_\Lambda)} d\mu_0} , \eqno{\text{(IV.35)}} $$
where $h_j \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$. As an immediate consequence of Theorem IV.10 we deduce:

COROLLARY IV.12. *Let $\Lambda$ be an open, bounded, regular region in $\mathbf{R}^2$. Let $h_1, \dots, h_r \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$. As $\delta \to 0$,*
$$ S_{\Lambda,\delta}^D(h_1, \dots, h_r) \longrightarrow S_\Lambda^D(h_1, \dots, h_r) . $$

Finally, we note that the half-Dirichlet lattice theory converges as $\delta \to 0$. We adopt the second point of view as explained in § II.6; i.e., we realize the noninteracting lattice Dirichlet theory in terms of the Dirichlet measure
$$ d\mu_{\Lambda,\delta}^D = (2\pi)^{-N/2} |A^\Lambda|^{1/2} \delta^N e^{-(1/2)\delta^2 q \cdot A^\Lambda q} dq $$
where $q_n = \phi_\delta(n), n = 1, 2, \dots, N$, are the field variables in $\Lambda$. The (smeared) lattice cutoff Schwinger functions with half-Dirichlet B.C. are (cf. (II.91))
$$ S_{\Lambda,\delta}^{HD}(h_1, \dots, h_r) = \frac{\int \phi_\delta(h_1) \dots \phi_\delta(h_r) e^{-U_\delta(\chi_\Lambda)} d\mu_{\Lambda,\delta}^D}{\int e^{-U_\delta(\chi_\Lambda)} d\mu_{\Lambda,\delta}^D} . \eqno{\text{(IV.36)}} $$
Here $U_\delta(\chi_\Lambda)$ is defined by (IV.9), that is, in terms of Wick powers $:\phi_\delta(n)^r:$ rather than $:\phi_\delta(n)^r:_D$. Let $P(X) = \sum_{r=0}^{2m} a_r X^r$. By Lemma V.27
$$ :\phi_\delta(n)^r: = \sum_{j=0}^{[r/2]} \left\{ \begin{matrix} r \\ j \end{matrix} \right\} (-B_\delta(n))^j :\phi_\delta^D(n)^{r-2j}:_D \eqno{\text{(IV.37)}} $$
where $\left\{ \begin{matrix} r \\ j \end{matrix} \right\} = r! / j! (r-2j)! 2^j$, and $B_\delta(n) = C_{n,n} - C_{n,n}^{\Lambda,D}$ (cf. (IV.15) and (IV.29)). It is convenient to regard the lattice cutoffs in $B_\delta(n)$ and $\phi_\delta^D(n)$ as being independent of one another and so we write by (IV.37)
$$ U_{\delta,\delta'}(\chi_\Lambda) = U_\delta^D(\chi_\Lambda) + \sum_{r=2}^{2m} a_r \sum_{j=1}^{[r/2]} \left\{ \begin{matrix} r \\ j \end{matrix} \right\} \sum_{n\delta \in \Lambda} \delta^2 (-g_{\delta'}(n\delta))^j :\phi_\delta^D(n)^{r-2j}:_D $$

***

# Page 16 (Journal Page 205)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 205

where $U_{\delta,\delta'}(\chi_\Lambda) = U_\delta(\chi_\Lambda)$ and $g_{\delta'}(x) = B_{\delta'}(n)$ if $x \in \Lambda$ is in the lattice square centered at $n\delta'$. By Theorem IV.10, when $\delta' \to 0$
$$ U_{\delta,\delta'}(\chi_\Lambda) \longrightarrow V_\delta = U_\delta^D(\chi_\Lambda) + \sum_{r=2}^{2m} a_r \sum_{j=1}^{[r/2]} \left\{ \begin{matrix} r \\ j \end{matrix} \right\} \int_\Lambda (-\delta G_\delta(x))^j :\phi(x)^{r-2j}:_D dx $$
in each $L^p(Q, d\mu_\Lambda^D), p < \infty$. Moreover, $\exp(-U_{\delta,\delta'}(\chi_\Lambda)) \to \exp(-V_\delta)$ in $L^p$, $p < \infty$. In addition, one can show by using the ideas of Lemma II.37 and Theorem II.38 that this convergence is uniform in $\delta$. Now let $\delta \to 0$. For $x \in \Lambda, g_\delta(x) \to \delta G(x)$ as defined in Lemma II.37(i), where the convergence takes place in $L^p(\Lambda), p < \infty$. Thus by Theorem II.38(iv), we have in each $L^p(Q, d\mu^D)$ for $p < \infty$,
$$ V_\delta \longrightarrow U^D(\chi_\Lambda) + \sum_{r=2}^{2m} a_r \sum_{j=1}^{[r/2]} \left\{ \begin{matrix} r \\ j \end{matrix} \right\} \int_\Lambda (-\delta G(x))^j :\phi(x)^{r-2j}:_D dx = U_\Lambda $$
by (II.88). Moreover, $e^{-V_\delta} \to e^{-U_\Lambda}$ in each $L^p, p < \infty$. We have thus verified:

THEOREM IV.13. *Let $\Lambda$ be an open, regular, log-normal region in $\mathbf{R}^2$, and let $h_1, \dots, h_r \in C_0^\infty(\mathbf{R}^2)$. As $\delta \to 0$*
$$ S_{\Lambda,\delta}^{HD}(h_1, \dots, h_r) \longrightarrow S_\Lambda^{HD}(h_1, \dots, h_r) . $$

IV.4. *The lattice Theory as an Ising Ferromagnet.* E. Nelson [69] has pointed out a useful interpretation of the lattice model which helps explain why we have been able to establish the correlation inequalities of the next section. Assume that the boundary matrix $B_{\partial\Lambda}$ is nonnegative, as we have proved it is in the case of free or Dirichlet B.C. Then the free part of the measure (IV.26) can be written
$$ \exp \left( \frac{1}{2} \sum_{n \ne n'} |B_{nn'}| q_n q_{n'} \right) \prod_n e^{-(1/2)B_{nn} q_n^2} d q_n , $$
where we have made use of the fact that $B_{nn'} \le 0$ if $n \ne n'$. Thus we see that the free measure gives a Gibbs state for a set of Gaussian spins with a ferromagnetic interaction Hamiltonian $H = (1/2) \sum_{n \ne n'} B_{nn'} q_n q_{n'}$. In fact by considering the entire array of spins, we conclude that the free lattice theory is an infinite array of Gaussian spins with nearest neighbour Ising interactions of ferromagnetic type. The extent to which the Gaussian model simulates the actual Ising model was discussed in 1952 by Berlin and Kac [4].

With the interaction turned on (“perturbed Gaussian model”) we have already noted that the local interaction does not change the coupling between spin sites but only the distribution of each uncoupled spin. An in-

***

# Page 17 (Journal Page 206)

206 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

teracting lattice Markov field theory is thus an array of continuous spins with ferromagnetic pair interactions between spins.

We warn the reader of a clash between the natural terminologies of field theory and statistical mechanics: What we have called Dirichlet B.C. ($B_{\partial\Lambda} = 0$) corresponds in the Ising model picture of the lattice theory to what would be called “free B.C.” in statistical mechanics. Our “free B.C.” ($B_{\partial\Lambda}$ given by Theorem IV.7) are very different. It is useful to bear this difference in mind when trying to understand the monotonicity of the Dirichlet states ([69] and § V.4).

## V. Correlation inequalities

In this section we wish to prove and apply correlation inequalities of the type that have been so useful in statistical mechanics [38], [85]. These inequalities have the general form of a positivity statement:
(G-I) $\langle A \rangle \ge 0$;
or a statement of positive correlation:
(G-II and FKG) $\langle AB \rangle \ge \langle A \rangle \langle B \rangle$.

Here $\langle \cdot \rangle$ denotes expectation on some space such that $\langle I \rangle = 1$, $A$ and $B$ are suitably chosen observables on this space, G-I and G-II refer to Griffiths' first and second inequalities respectively, and FKG refers to the inequalities of Fortuin-Kasteleyn-Ginibre [20]. The proof of G-I and G-II in our case (Theorems V.1-2, 7-9 below) uses ideas developed in a beautiful analysis of general Griffiths' inequalities by Ginibre [27].

In the first subsection, motivated by the form of the lattice approximation of § IV, we prove inequalities for a class of measures on $\mathbf{R}^n$ which we call “normally perturbed Gaussian measures of ferromagnetic type”. It is then easy (§ V.2) to obtain correlation inequalities for the $P(\phi)_2$ field theory. After discussing the possibilities for correlation inequalities involving Wick powers (§ V.3), we turn to applications in § V.4.

Perhaps the most significant consequence of the $P(\phi)_2$ correlation inequalities to date is Nelson's proof ([69] and see Theorem V.20 below) that if $P$ is even and the coupling constant $\lambda > 0$ arbitrary, then the non-coincident Schwinger functions with half-Dirichlet B.C. converge monotonically as the volume cutoff is removed. By “non-coincident Schwinger functions” we mean the smeared Schwinger functions whose smearing functions have pairwise disjoint supports.

V.1. *Gaussian Measures of Ferromagnetic Type.* The form (IV.1) of the

***

# Page 18 (Journal Page 207)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 207

measure in the lattice approximation leads us to a consideration of these measures on $\mathbf{R}^n$:

*Definitions:* A measure $d\mu_0 = e^{-(1/2)\vec{x} \cdot A \vec{x}} d^n x$ where $A$ is a positive definite matrix with nonpositive off-diagonal elements is called a *Gaussian measure of ferromagnetic type*. If $F_1, \dots, F_n$ are bounded, continuous, positive functions on $\mathbf{R}$, the measure $d\mu(x) = F_1(x_1) \dots F_n(x_n) d\mu_0(x)$ is called a *normally perturbed Gaussian measure of ferromagnetic type*, or, for short, a *ferromagnetic measure*. If each $F_i$ is an even function of $x_i$, we say that $\mu$ is an *even ferromagnetic measure*.

If $\nu$ is any of the above measures, we define the normalized expectation value of a function $f$ on $\mathbf{R}^n$ as
$$ \langle f \rangle_\nu = \int f(x) d\nu(x) \Big/ \int d\nu(x) . \eqno{\text{(V.1)}} $$

Finally, if $f$ satisfies $f(x) \ge f(y)$ whenever $x_i \ge y_i$ for $i = 1, \dots, n$, we say that $f$ is an increasing function on $\mathbf{R}^n$ and we write $f \uparrow$.

With these definitions, we are able to state the main results of this subsection:

THEOREM V.1. *If $\mu$ is an even ferromagnetic measure and $j_1, \dots, j_n$ are nonnegative integers, then*
$$ \langle x_1^{j_1} \dots x_n^{j_n} \rangle_\mu \ge 0 . $$

THEOREM V.2. *If $\mu$ is an even ferromagnetic measure and $j_1, \dots, k_n$ are nonnegative integers, then*
$$ \langle x_1^{j_1+k_1} \dots x_n^{j_n+k_n} \rangle_\mu \ge \langle x_1^{j_1} \dots x_n^{j_n} \rangle_\mu \langle x_1^{k_1} \dots x_n^{k_n} \rangle_\mu . $$

THEOREM V.3. *Let $\mu$ be any ferromagnetic measure. Then*
$$ \langle fg \rangle_\mu \ge \langle f \rangle_\mu \langle g \rangle_\mu $$
*if $f$ and $g$ are continuous, polynomially bounded functions with $f, g \uparrow$.*

*Remarks* 1. H. Leff [59] has proved Theorems 1 and 2 by explicit computation under the special hypothesis that $F_1 = \dots = F_n = 1$, and the weaker condition on $A$ that $A^{-1}$ be positive definite with nonnegative elements.
2. F. Spitzer (private communication) has also proved Theorem 3.
3. These three theorems are of type G-I, G-II, and FKG respectively.
4. The assumptions on the perturbing functions $F_j$ can be weakened somewhat. For instance, $F_j$ need not be bounded but must merely be of order 2 and type less than any eigenvalue of $A$; i.e., $F_j(x) = O(e^{ax^2})$ where $a < \min \sigma(A)$. At the end of this subsection we consider the extent to which we can relax the assumption that $\mu$ be even in the first two theorems.

***

# Page 19 (Journal Page 208)

208 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

Typically the functions $F_i$ are of the form $e^{-\beta P_i}$ where $P_i(x)$ is a polynomial. Since the earliest simplifications of Griffiths' work [36], [53], [26], the standard method of proving correlation inequalities has been to expand the exponential. In our case this does not work: first, the expansion generally does not converge and, secondly, the inequalities are not true order by order in $\beta$. Instead we shall expand the *off-diagonal part* of the Gaussian measure. Thus we take as our base measure
$$ d\nu = F_1(x_1) \dots F_n(x_n) e^{-(1/2)\vec{x} \cdot A_0 \vec{x}} d^n x , \eqno{\text{(V.2)}} $$
where $A_0$ is the diagonal part of $A$, the matrix whose off-diagonal elements vanish and whose diagonal elements are the same as $A$'s We also define, as the interpolation between $d\nu$ and $d\mu$,
$$ d\mu^{(\lambda)} = F_1(x_1) \dots F_n(x_n) e^{-(1/2)\vec{x} \cdot A_\lambda \vec{x}} d^n x \eqno{\text{(V.3)}} $$
where $\lambda \in [0, 1]$ and $A_\lambda = \lambda A + (1 - \lambda)A_0$. An important role is played by:

LEMMA V.4. *If $\mu$ is an even ferromagnetic measure, then the function $g(\lambda) = \int x_1^{k_1} \dots x_n^{k_n} d\mu^{(\lambda)}$ is analytic in a circle with center at the origin and radius greater than 1.*

*Proof.* Since $A$ is invertible and positive definite, so are $A_0$ and thus $A_\lambda$ if $0 \le \lambda \le 1$. It follows that $g(\lambda)$ is analytic in a complex neighbourhood of $[0, 1]$. In particular $g$ is analytic about $\lambda = 0$. Now the $m^{th}$ derivative
$$ g^{(m)}(0) = \int [\sum_{i \ne j} (-a_{ij})x_i x_j]^m x_1^{k_1} \dots x_n^{k_n} d\nu \ge 0 $$
since each $a_{ij} \le 0$ and each $F_i$ is even and positive. But an analytic function with nonnegative Taylor series at the origin must have its nearest singularity on the positive real axis. Thus there is an $\epsilon > 0$ such that the Taylor series for $g$ at 0 converges if $|\lambda| < 1 + \epsilon$. $\square$

*Proof of Theorem V.1.* By the lemma, $g(1) = \sum_0^\infty g^{(m)}(0)/m! \ge 0$. $\square$

*Remark.* We owe to E. Lieb (private communication) the observation that Theorem V.1 is actually equivalent to the usual statement of Griffiths' first inequality: For ferromagnetic spin systems, G-I asserts that the (un-normalized) expectation
$$ \langle \sigma_1^{k_1} \dots \sigma_n^{k_n} \rangle_\beta \equiv \sum_{\sigma_j = \pm 1} \sigma_1^{k_1} \dots \sigma_n^{k_n} \exp \left( \sum_{i \ne j} J_{ij}(\beta) \sigma_i \sigma_j \right) \ge 0 \eqno{\text{(V.4)}} $$
provided the couplings are ferromagnetic; i.e., $J_{ij} = J_{ji} \ge 0$ for $i \ne j$. If we set $\sigma_i = \text{sgn } x_i$, then (V.4) implies
$$ \int x_1^{k_1} \dots x_n^{k_n} d\mu \equiv \int_{\mathbf{R}^n_+} x_1^{k_1} \dots x_n^{k_n} F_1(x_1) \dots F_n(x_n) \langle \sigma_1^{k_1} \dots \sigma_n^{k_n} \rangle_{\vec{x} \cdot A \vec{x}} d^n x \ge 0 $$

***

# Page 20 (Journal Page 209)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 209

where $\mathbf{R}^n_+ = \{x \mid x_i > 0\}$ and $J_{ij}(x) = A_{ij} |x_i| |x_j|$.

Conversely, given the ferromagnetic couplings $J_{ij}$ ($i \ne j$) we can find a matrix $A$ with $A_{ij} = J_{ij}$ if $i \ne j$ and $A_{ii} = a$ such that $A$ is positive definite. Taking $F_i(x_i) = \delta(x_i + 1) + \delta(x_i - 1)$ (or a limit of smooth approximations to this function) we recover the spin inequality from Theorem V.1.

In his general analysis of Griffiths' inequalities, Ginibre [27] isolates a condition (Q3) that the measure and observables should satisfy to yield correlation inequalities. In our case (Q3) is just:

LEMMA V.5. *Let $d\nu$ be the even measure (V.2) and let $P_1, \dots, P_m$ be polynomials in $x \in \mathbf{R}^n$ with nonnegative coefficients. Then for any choice of the plus or minus signs,*
$$ \int \prod_{i=1}^m (P_i(x) \pm P_i(y)) d\nu(x) d\nu(y) \ge 0 . \eqno{\text{(V.5)}} $$

*Proof.* Clearly it is sufficient to prove the lemma if each $P_i$ is a monomial of the form $x_1^{i_1} \dots x_n^{i_n}$. First note that if $n = 1$,
$$ \int (x - y)^i (x + y)^j d\nu(x) d\nu(y) \ge 0 \eqno{\text{(V.6)}} $$
for all nonnegative integers $i, j$. For if $i + j$ is odd the integral vanishes by $(x, y) \to (-x, -y)$ symmetry. If $i$ is odd, it vanishes by $(x, y) \to (y, x)$ symmetry. Thus the integral can be non-zero only if $i$ and $j$ are both even, in which case the integrand is positive. Since
$$ (x^i \pm y^i) = 2^{-i+1} \sum_{j \in \{ \text{even/odd} \}} \binom{i}{j} (x + y)^{i-j}(x - y)^j , $$
we conclude from (V.6) that
$$ \int (x_1^{i_1} \pm y_1^{i_1}) \dots (x_n^{i_m} \pm y_n^{i_m}) d\nu(x) d\nu(y) \ge 0 \eqno{\text{(V.7)}} $$
for any choice of $\pm$ from each pair and any nonnegative integers $i_1, \dots, i_m$.
Finally, if $n > 1$ we appeal to the product nature of the measure $d\nu$ and deduce (V.5) from (V.7) by repeated use of
$$ \begin{aligned} h_1(x_1)h_2(x_2) \pm h_1(y_1)h_2(y_2) &= \frac{1}{2} [h_1(x_1) + h_1(y_1)][h_2(x_2) \pm h_2(y_2)] \\ &\quad + \frac{1}{2} [h_1(x_1) - h_1(y_1)][h_2(x_2) \mp h_2(y_2)] . \end{aligned} \square $$

*Proof of Theorem V.2.* We must show that
$$ \int (x^{(j)} - y^{(j)})(x^{(k)} - y^{(k)}) d\mu(x) d\mu(y) \ge 0 \eqno{\text{(V.8)}} $$

This is a 1-1 transcription of **Part II** of the paper, continuing from journal page 210 through 219 (Screenshots 21 through 30).

***

# Page 21 (Journal Page 210)

210 F. GUERRA, L. ROSEN, AND B. SIMON

where $x^{(j)} = x_1^{j_1} \dots x_n^{j_n}$. By Lemma V.4, the integral (V.8) can be evaluated by a power series expansion in the off-diagonal part of the Gaussian measure. But each term in this expansion
$$ \frac{1}{m!} \int (x^{(j)} - y^{(j)})(x^{(k)} - y^{(k)}) [\sum_{i \ne j} (-a_{ij})(x_i x_j + y_i y_j)]^m d\nu(x) d\nu(y) $$
is nonnegative by Lemma V.5. $\square$

Finally, to prove Theorem V.3 we observe that a ferromagnetic measure satisfies the FKG condition:

LEMMA V.6. *Let $d\mu(x) = G(x) d^n x$ be a ferromagnetic measure on $\mathbf{R}^n$. Then*
(V.9) $$ G(x \wedge y) G(x \vee y) \ge G(x) G(y) . $$

*Notation.* Under the ordering $x \ge y$ if $x_i \ge y_i, i = 1, \dots, n, \mathbf{R}^n$ is a distributive lattice with the operations $\vee$ and $\wedge$ given by $(x \vee y)_i = \max(x_i, y_i)$ and $(x \wedge y)_i = \min(x_i, y_i)$.

*Proof.* By definition $G(x) = F(x) e^{-\frac{1}{2} x \cdot A x}$. Since $F(x) = F_1(x_1) \dots F_n(x_n)$ $F(x \vee y) F(x \wedge y) = F(x) F(y)$. Moreover,
$$ \begin{aligned} &(x \wedge y) \cdot A (x \wedge y) + (x \vee y) \cdot A (x \vee y) - x \cdot A x - y \cdot A y \\ &\quad = 2 \sum_{i,j} a_{ij} (x_i \wedge y_i)(x_j \wedge y_j) + \dots - x_i x_j - y_i y_j) \\ &\quad = 2 \sum_{x_i < y_i, x_j > y_j} a_{ij} (x_i - y_i)(y_j - x_j) \le 0 \end{aligned} $$
since $a_{ij} \le 0$ if $i \ne j$. $\square$

*Proof of Theorem V.3.* By Lemma V.6 we see that the theorem is just a continuum version of the usual FKG inequality [20], [49]. To reduce the theorem to the case of a *finite* distributive lattice, we simply approximate $\langle f \rangle_\mu$ by
$$ \langle f \rangle_N = \frac{\sum_{|j_i| \le N^2} f(j_1/N, \dots, j_n/N) G(j_1/N, \dots, j_n/N) N^{-n}}{\sum_{|j_i| \le N^2} G(j_1/N, \dots, j_n/N) N^{-n}} . $$
Then by FKG, $\langle fg \rangle_N \ge \langle f \rangle_N \langle g \rangle_N$, and letting $N \to \infty$ yields the theorem. $\square$

We conclude this subsection with a discussion of the necessity of the condition that $\mu$ be even in Theorems V.1 and V.2. This condition cannot be dropped completely as the following example shows:

*Example.* Consider the ferromagnetic measure $d\mu(x) = e^{(-1/2)x \cdot A x - \lambda \cdot x}$, i.e., $F_i(x_i) = e^{-\lambda_i x_i}, i = 1, \dots, n$. Setting $m = -A^{-1} \lambda$, we see that

***

# Page 22 (Journal Page 211)

$P(\phi)_2$ EUCLIDEAN QUANTUM FIELD THEORY 211

$$ \frac{1}{2} x \cdot A x + \lambda \cdot x = \frac{1}{2} (x - m) \cdot A (x - m) + \frac{1}{2} m \cdot \lambda , $$
so that the effect of the linear perturbation is to change the means but not the covariance of $x_i$'s. From the joint characteristic function of the $x_i$'s,
$$ \begin{aligned} \chi(q) &= \int e^{iq \cdot x} d\mu(x) \Big/ \int d\mu(x) \\ &= \exp \left[ im \cdot q - \frac{1}{2} q \cdot A^{-1} q \right] , \end{aligned} $$
we can read off the various expectations of products of $x_i$'s. For example,
$$ \langle x_1 x_2 \rangle_\mu = \frac{1}{i^2} \frac{\partial^2 \chi}{\partial q_1 \partial q_2} \bigg|_{q=0} = (A^{-1})_{12} + m_1 m_2 , $$
$$ \langle x_1 x_2 x_3 \rangle_\mu = m_1 m_2 m_3 + m_1 (A^{-1})_{23} + m_2 (A^{-1})_{13} + m_3 (A^{-1})_{12} , $$
and so on. Therefore,
(V.10) $$ \langle x_1 x_2 x_3 \rangle_\mu - \langle x_1 x_2 \rangle_\mu \langle x_3 \rangle_\mu = m_1 (A^{-1})_{23} + m_2 (A^{-1})_{13} . $$
Now we know by Theorem A.1 that $A^{-1}$ always has nonnegative entries; certainly by a specific choice of $A$ we can arrange that $A^{-1}$ have strictly positive entries. Consequently the right side of (V.10) is given by an expression of the form $\sum c_i \lambda_i$ where $c_i < 0$. We conclude that G-II holds if all the $\lambda_i \le 0$, but fails if $\lambda_i > 0$ (see Remark 3 after Theorem V.10).

On the other hand, it is formally clear that Theorem V.1 will still hold if $F$ even is replaced by the assumption that
(V.11) $$ F(x) = G(x) \sum_{i=0}^\infty c_i x^i $$
where $G(x)$ is even and $c_i \ge 0$. From the role of Lemma V.5, we see that Theorem V.2 is formally true if in addition products of $F$'s satisfy
(V.12) $$ \prod_{j=1}^n F_j(x_j) F_j(y_j) = \prod_{j=1}^n G_j(x_j) G_j(y_j) \sum_k \prod_{i=1}^m (P_{ki}(x) \pm P_{ki}(y)) , $$
where $G_j(x_j)$ is an even function of $x_j$ and the $P_{ki}$ are polynomials in $x \in \mathbf{R}^n$ with nonnegative coefficients. Rather than attempting to characterize such $F_j$ (not to mention proving the convergence of the series!), we are content to notice that one important class of $F_j$'s satisfying (V.11) and (V.12) is the class
(V.13) $$ F_j(x_j) = e^{-P_j(x_j) + Q_j(x_j)} $$
